<p><b>Android</b> (stylized as <b>android</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Mobile_operating_system" title="Mobile operating system">mobile operating system</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, based on the <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">Linux kernel</a> and designed primarily for <a href="/wiki/Touchscreen" title="Touchscreen">touchscreen</a> mobile devices such as <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablets</a>. Android's <a href="/wiki/User_interface" title="User interface">user interface</a> is mainly based on <a href="/wiki/Direct_manipulation_interface" title="Direct manipulation interface">direct manipulation</a>, using touch gestures that loosely correspond to real-world actions, such as swiping, tapping and pinching, to manipulate on-screen objects, along with a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_keyboard" title="Virtual keyboard">virtual keyboard</a> for text input. In addition to touchscreen devices, Google has further developed <a href="/wiki/Android_TV" title="Android TV">Android TV</a> for televisions, <a href="/wiki/Android_Auto" title="Android Auto">Android Auto</a> for cars, and <a href="/wiki/Android_Wear" title="Android Wear">Android Wear</a> for wrist watches, each with a specialized user interface. Variants of Android are also used on <a href="/wiki/Laptop" title="Laptop">notebooks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">game consoles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Digital_camera" title="Digital camera">digital cameras</a>, and other electronics.</p>
<p>Android has the largest <a href="/wiki/Installed_base" title="Installed base">installed base</a> of all operating systems (OS) of any kind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[b]</a></sup> Android has been the best selling OS on tablets since 2013, and on smartphones it is dominant by any metric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manjoomurky_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-manjoomurky-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dominating_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-dominating-18">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially developed by Android, Inc., which Google bought in 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidInc_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidInc-19">[17]</a></sup> Android was unveiled in 2007 along with the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Open_Handset_Alliance" title="Open Handset Alliance">Open Handset Alliance</a> – a consortium of <a href="/wiki/Computer_hardware" title="Computer hardware">hardware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a>, and telecommunication companies devoted to advancing <a href="/wiki/Open_standard" title="Open standard">open standards</a> for mobile devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidAnnouncement_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidAnnouncement-20">[18]</a></sup> As of July 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Android_(operating_system)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Google_Play" title="Google Play">Google Play</a> store has had over one million Android applications ("apps") published –  including many "business-class apps"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Infoworld2016_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Infoworld2016-21">[19]</a></sup> that rival competing mobile platforms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Infoworld2015_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Infoworld2015-22">[20]</a></sup> –  and over 50 billion applications downloaded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phonearena1_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-phonearena1-23">[21]</a></sup> An April–May 2013 survey of mobile application developers found that 71% of developers create applications for Android,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-visionmobile1_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-visionmobile1-24">[22]</a></sup> and a 2015 survey found that 40% of full-time professional developers see Android as their priority target platform, which is comparable to <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>'s <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a> on 37% with both platforms far above others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> In September 2015, Android had 1.4 billion monthly active devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android's <a href="/wiki/Source_code" title="Source code">source code</a> is released by Google under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_source" title="Open source">open source</a> licenses, although most Android devices ultimately ship with a combination of open source and proprietary software, including proprietary software required for accessing Google services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup> Android is popular with technology companies that require a ready-made, low-cost and customizable operating system for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-tech" title="High-tech">high-tech</a> devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars5th_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars5th-27">[25]</a></sup> Its open nature has encouraged a large community of developers and enthusiasts to use the open-source code as a foundation for community-driven projects, which deliver updates to older devices, add new features for advanced users<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apolroms_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-apolroms-28">[26]</a></sup> or bring Android to devices originally shipped with other operating systems. The success of Android has made it a target for patent (and copyright) litigation as part of the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Smartphone_patent_wars" title="Smartphone patent wars">smartphone wars</a>" between technology companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnet2011_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnet2011-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tomsguide_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-tomsguide-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Android_version_history" title="Android version history">Android version history</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Android_logo_(2007-2014).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="60" data-file-width="445" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Android_logo_%282007-2014%29.svg/250px-Android_logo_%282007-2014%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Android_logo_%282007-2014%29.svg/375px-Android_logo_%282007-2014%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Android_logo_%282007-2014%29.svg/500px-Android_logo_%282007-2014%29.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Android_logo_(2007-2014).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Former Android logo wordmark (2007-2014)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Android, Inc. was founded in <a href="/wiki/Palo_Alto,_California" title="Palo Alto, California">Palo Alto, California</a> in October 2003 by <a href="/wiki/Andy_Rubin" title="Andy Rubin">Andy Rubin</a> (co-founder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danger_(company)" title="Danger (company)">Danger</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndyRubin_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-AndyRubin-31">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rich_Miner" title="Rich Miner">Rich Miner</a> (co-founder of Wildfire Communications, Inc.),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-32">[30]</a></sup> Nick Sears (once VP at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-Mobile_USA" title="T-Mobile USA">T-Mobile</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> and Chris White (headed design and interface development at <a href="/wiki/MSN_TV" title="MSN TV">WebTV</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidInc_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidInc-19">[17]</a></sup>) to develop, in Rubin's words, "smarter mobile devices that are more aware of its owner's location and preferences".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidInc_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidInc-19">[17]</a></sup> The early intentions of the company were to develop an advanced operating system for <a href="/wiki/Digital_camera" title="Digital camera">digital cameras</a>. Though, when it was realized that the market for the devices was not large enough, the company diverted its efforts toward producing a smartphone operating system that would rival <a href="/wiki/Symbian" title="Symbian">Symbian</a> and Microsoft <a href="/wiki/Windows_Mobile" title="Windows Mobile">Windows Mobile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> Despite the past accomplishments of the founders and early employees, Android Inc. operated secretly, revealing only that it was working on software for mobile phones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidInc_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidInc-19">[17]</a></sup> That same year, Rubin ran out of money. <a href="/wiki/Steve_Perlman" title="Steve Perlman">Steve Perlman</a>, a close friend of Rubin, brought him $10,000 in cash in an envelope and refused a stake in the company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StevePerlman_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-StevePerlman-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2005, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> acquired Android Inc. for at least $50 million, whose key employees, including Rubin, Miner and White, stayed at the company after the acquisition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manjoomurky_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-manjoomurky-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidInc_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidInc-19">[17]</a></sup> Not much was known about Android Inc. at the time, but many assumed that Google was planning to enter the mobile phone market with this move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidInc_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidInc-19">[17]</a></sup> At Google, the team led by Rubin developed a mobile device platform powered by the <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">Linux kernel</a>. Google marketed the platform to handset makers and <a href="/wiki/Mobile_network_operator" title="Mobile network operator">carriers</a> on the promise of providing a flexible, upgradeable system. Google had lined up a series of hardware component and software partners and signaled to carriers that it was open to various degrees of cooperation on their part.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EngadgetMobileOS_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-EngadgetMobileOS-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DT_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-DT-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Speculation about Google's intention to enter the mobile communications market continued to build through December 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKay_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKay-39">[37]</a></sup> An earlier prototype codenamed "Sooner" had a closer resemblance to a <a href="/wiki/BlackBerry" title="BlackBerry">BlackBerry</a> phone, with no touchscreen, and a physical, <a href="/wiki/QWERTY" title="QWERTY">QWERTY</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_keyboard" title="Computer keyboard">keyboard</a>, but was later re-engineered to support a touchscreen, to compete with other announced devices such as the 2006 <a href="/wiki/LG_Prada" title="LG Prada">LG Prada</a> and 2007 Apple <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> In September 2007, <i><a href="/wiki/InformationWeek" title="InformationWeek">InformationWeek</a></i> covered an <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Evalueserve&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Evalueserve (page does not exist)">Evalueserve</a> study reporting that Google had filed several patent applications in the area of mobile telephony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IWpatents_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-IWpatents-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MNpatents_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-MNpatents-43">[41]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Press_Conference_for_the_Launch_of_Nexus_7_on_September_27,_2012_in_Seoul_from_acrofan_3.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="335" data-file-width="599" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/A_Press_Conference_for_the_Launch_of_Nexus_7_on_September_27%2C_2012_in_Seoul_from_acrofan_3.JPG/220px-A_Press_Conference_for_the_Launch_of_Nexus_7_on_September_27%2C_2012_in_Seoul_from_acrofan_3.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/A_Press_Conference_for_the_Launch_of_Nexus_7_on_September_27%2C_2012_in_Seoul_from_acrofan_3.JPG/330px-A_Press_Conference_for_the_Launch_of_Nexus_7_on_September_27%2C_2012_in_Seoul_from_acrofan_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/A_Press_Conference_for_the_Launch_of_Nexus_7_on_September_27%2C_2012_in_Seoul_from_acrofan_3.JPG/440px-A_Press_Conference_for_the_Launch_of_Nexus_7_on_September_27%2C_2012_in_Seoul_from_acrofan_3.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Press_Conference_for_the_Launch_of_Nexus_7_on_September_27,_2012_in_Seoul_from_acrofan_3.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eric_Schmidt" title="Eric Schmidt">Eric Schmidt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andy_Rubin" title="Andy Rubin">Andy Rubin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Barra" title="Hugo Barra">Hugo Barra</a> at a 2012 press conference announcing Google's Nexus 7 tablet</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On November 5, 2007, the Open Handset Alliance, a <a href="/wiki/Consortium" title="Consortium">consortium</a> of technology companies including Google, device manufacturers such as <a href="/wiki/HTC" title="HTC">HTC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a>, wireless carriers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sprint_Nextel" title="Sprint Nextel">Sprint Nextel</a> and <a href="/wiki/T-Mobile" title="T-Mobile">T-Mobile</a>, and chipset makers such as <a href="/wiki/Qualcomm" title="Qualcomm">Qualcomm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas_Instruments" title="Texas Instruments">Texas Instruments</a>, unveiled itself, with a goal to develop <a href="/wiki/Open_standard" title="Open standard">open standards</a> for mobile devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidAnnouncement_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidAnnouncement-20">[18]</a></sup> That day, Android was unveiled as its first product, a mobile device <a href="/wiki/Computing_platform" title="Computing platform">platform</a> built on the <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">Linux kernel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidAnnouncement_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidAnnouncement-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kernels_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-kernels-44">[42]</a></sup> The first commercially available smartphone running Android was the <a href="/wiki/HTC_Dream" title="HTC Dream">HTC Dream</a>, released on October 22, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gizmodo-g1_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-gizmodo-g1-45">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2008, Android has seen <a href="/wiki/Android_version_history" title="Android version history">numerous updates</a> which have incrementally improved the operating system, adding new features and fixing bugs in previous releases. Each major release is named in alphabetical order after a dessert or sugary treat; for example, version 1.5 "Cupcake" was followed by 1.6 "Donut". In 2010, Google launched its <a href="/wiki/Google_Nexus" title="Google Nexus">Nexus</a> series of devices – a line of smartphones and tablets running the Android operating system, and built by manufacturing partners. HTC collaborated with Google to release the first Nexus smartphone,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Nexus_One" title="Nexus One">Nexus One</a>. Google has since updated the series with newer devices, such as the <a href="/wiki/Nexus_5" title="Nexus 5">Nexus 5</a> phone (made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LG" title="LG">LG</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nexus_7_(2013_version)" title="Nexus 7 (2013 version)">Nexus 7</a> tablet (made by <a href="/wiki/Asus" title="Asus">Asus</a>). Google releases the Nexus phones and tablets to act as their <a href="/wiki/Core_product" title="Core product">flagship</a> Android devices, demonstrating Android's latest software and hardware features. From 2013 until 2015, Google offered several <a href="/wiki/List_of_Google_Play_edition_devices" title="List of Google Play edition devices">Google Play Edition</a> devices over Google Play. While not carrying the Google Nexus branding, these were Google-customized Android phones and tablets that also ran the latest version of Android, free from manufacturer or carrier modifications.</p>
<p>From 2010 to 2013, <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Barra" title="Hugo Barra">Hugo Barra</a> served as product spokesperson, representing Android at press conferences and <a href="/wiki/Google_I/O" title="Google I/O">Google I/O</a>, Google’s annual developer-focused conference. Barra's product involvement included the entire Android ecosystem of software and hardware, including Honeycomb, Ice Cream Sandwich, Jelly Bean and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KitKat_(operating_system)" title="KitKat (operating system)">KitKat</a> operating system launches, the <a href="/wiki/Nexus_4" title="Nexus 4">Nexus 4</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nexus_5" title="Nexus 5">Nexus 5</a> smartphones, the <a href="/wiki/Nexus_7_(2012)" title="Nexus 7 (2012)">Nexus 7</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nexus_10" title="Nexus 10">Nexus 10</a> tablets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> and other related products such as <a href="/wiki/Google_Now" title="Google Now">Google Now</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wired-49">[47]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Google_Voice_Search" title="Google Voice Search">Google Voice Search</a>, Google’s speech recognition product comparable to <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Siri" title="Siri">Siri</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wired_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wired-49">[47]</a></sup> In 2013, Barra left the Android team for Chinese smartphone maker <a href="/wiki/Xiaomi" title="Xiaomi">Xiaomi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> The same year, <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a> announced in a blog post that Andy Rubin had moved from the Android division to take on new projects at Google.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> He was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Sundar_Pichai" title="Sundar Pichai">Sundar Pichai</a> who became the new head of Android and <a href="/wiki/Chrome_OS" title="Chrome OS">Chrome OS</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> and, later, by Hiroshi Lockheimer when Pichai became CEO of Google.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, Google launched <a href="/wiki/Android_One" title="Android One">Android One</a>, a line of smartphones mainly targeting customers in the developing world. In May 2015, Google announced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Project_Brillo" title="Project Brillo">Project Brillo</a> as a cut-down version of Android that uses its lower levels (excluding the <a href="/wiki/User_interface" title="User interface">user interface</a>), intended for the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_of_Things" title="Internet of Things">Internet of Things</a>" (IoT) <a href="/wiki/Embedded_system" title="Embedded system">embedded systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>University of Cambridge research in 2015, concluded that almost 90% of Android phones in use had known but unpatched <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Security_vulnerabilities" title="Security vulnerabilities">security vulnerabilities</a> due to lack of updates and support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Android_Vulnerabilities_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Android_Vulnerabilities-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waiting_for_Android.E2.80.99s_inevitable_security_Armageddon_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waiting_for_Android.E2.80.99s_inevitable_security_Armageddon-56">[54]</a></sup> In a year since (mid-2015) that report, well over a billion Android smartphones have been sold (more than the just over billion sold in 2014); and Android 5.0 (with better security) and later, went from 5.4% market share to currently over half, which means that the 90% number must be very outdated; those phones now very likely represent less than half of all Android phones. Recent devices do get security updates;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> Android 5.0 introduced an improved centralized update system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_features_in_Android" title="List of features in Android">List of features in Android</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interface">Interface</span></h3>
<p>Android's default user interface is mainly based on <a href="/wiki/Direct_manipulation_interface" title="Direct manipulation interface">direct manipulation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-touch_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-touch-61">[59]</a></sup> using touch inputs that loosely correspond to real-world actions, like swiping, tapping, pinching, and reverse pinching to manipulate on-screen objects, along with a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_keyboard" title="Virtual keyboard">virtual keyboard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-touch_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-touch-61">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Game_controller" title="Game controller">Game controllers</a> and full-size physical <a href="/wiki/Computer_keyboard" title="Computer keyboard">keyboards</a> are supported via <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth" title="Bluetooth">Bluetooth</a> or <a href="/wiki/USB" title="USB">USB</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> The response to user input is designed to be immediate and provides a fluid touch interface, often using the vibration capabilities of the device to provide <a href="/wiki/Haptic_technology" title="Haptic technology">haptic feedback</a> to the user. Internal hardware, such as <a href="/wiki/Accelerometer" title="Accelerometer">accelerometers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proximity_sensor" title="Proximity sensor">proximity sensors</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> are used by some applications to respond to additional user actions, for example adjusting the screen from portrait to landscape depending on how the device is oriented, or allowing the user to steer a vehicle in a racing game by rotating the device, simulating control of a <a href="/wiki/Steering_wheel" title="Steering wheel">steering wheel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android devices boot to the homescreen, the primary navigation and information "hub" on Android devices that is analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Desktop_metaphor" title="Desktop metaphor">desktop</a> found on personal computers. (Android also runs on regular personal computers, as described <a href="#DESKTOP">below</a>). Android homescreens are typically made up of app icons and <a href="/wiki/Software_widget" title="Software widget">widgets</a>; app icons launch the associated app, whereas widgets display live, auto-updating content, such as the weather forecast, the user's email inbox, or a <a href="/wiki/News_ticker" title="News ticker">news ticker</a> directly on the homescreen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> A homescreen may be made up of several pages, between which the user can swipe back and forth, though Android's homescreen interface is heavily customisable, allowing users to adjust the look and feel of the devices to their tastes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> Third-party apps available on <a href="/wiki/Google_Play" title="Google Play">Google Play</a> and other app stores can extensively re-<a href="/wiki/Theme_(computing)" title="Theme (computing)">theme</a> the homescreen, and even mimic the look of other operating systems, such as <a href="/wiki/Windows_Phone" title="Windows Phone">Windows Phone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup> Most manufacturers, and some <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone_operator" title="Mobile phone operator">wireless carriers</a>, customise the look and feel of their Android devices to differentiate themselves from their competitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup> Applications that handle interactions with the homescreen are called "launchers" because they, among other purposes, launch the applications installed on a device.</p>
<p>Along the top of the screen is a status bar, showing information about the device and its connectivity. This status bar can be "pulled" down to reveal a notification screen where apps display important information or updates, such as a newly received email or SMS text, in a way that does not immediately interrupt or inconvenience the user.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ui-overview_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-ui-overview-69">[67]</a></sup> Notifications are persistent until read by tapping it, which opens the relevant app, or dismissed by sliding it off the screen. Beginning on Android 4.1, "expanded notifications" can display expanded details or additional functionality; for instance, a music player can display playback controls, and a "missed call" notification provides buttons for calling back or sending the caller an SMS message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-notifications_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-notifications-70">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android provides the ability to run applications that change the default launcher, and hence the appearance and externally visible behaviour of Android. These appearance changes include a multi-page dock or no dock, and many more changes to fundamental features of the user interface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Android_software_development" title="Android software development">Android software development</a> and <a href="/wiki/Google_Play" title="Google Play">Google Play</a></div>
<p>Applications ("<a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">apps</a>"), which extend the functionality of devices, are written using the <a href="/wiki/Android_software_development" title="Android software development">Android software development</a> kit (SDK) and, often, the <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a> programming language that has complete access to the Android APIs. Java may be combined with <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>/<a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a>, together with a choice of non-default runtimes that allow better C++ support;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Go_(programming_language)" title="Go (programming language)">Go</a> programming language is also supported since its version 1.4, which can also be used exclusively although with a restricted set of Android APIs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> The SDK includes a comprehensive set of development tools,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> including a <a href="/wiki/Debugger" title="Debugger">debugger</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Software_library" title="Software library">software libraries</a>, a handset <a href="/wiki/Emulator" title="Emulator">emulator</a> based on <a href="/wiki/QEMU" title="QEMU">QEMU</a>, documentation, sample code, and tutorials. Initially, Google's supported <a href="/wiki/Integrated_development_environment" title="Integrated development environment">integrated development environment</a> (IDE) was <a href="/wiki/Eclipse_(software)" title="Eclipse (software)">Eclipse</a> using the Android Development Tools (ADT) plugin; in December 2014, Google released <a href="/wiki/Android_Studio" title="Android Studio">Android Studio</a>, based on <a href="/wiki/IntelliJ_IDEA" title="IntelliJ IDEA">IntelliJ IDEA</a>, as its primary IDE for Android application development. Other development tools are available, including a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_development_kit" title="Native development kit">native development kit</a> (NDK) for applications or extensions in C or C++, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_App_Inventor" title="Google App Inventor">Google App Inventor</a>, a visual environment for novice programmers, and various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiple_phone_web_based_application_framework" title="Multiple phone web based application framework">cross platform mobile web applications frameworks</a>. In January 2014, Google unveiled an framework based on <a href="/wiki/Apache_Cordova" title="Apache Cordova">Apache Cordova</a> for porting <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Chrome</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HTML_5" title="HTML 5">HTML 5</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Web_app" title="Web app">web applications</a> to Android, wrapped in a native application shell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android has a growing selection of third-party applications, which can be acquired by users by downloading and installing the application's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/APK_(file_format)" title="APK (file format)">APK</a> (Android application package) file, or by downloading them using an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Application_store" title="Application store">application store</a> program that allows users to <a href="/wiki/Package_manager" title="Package manager">install, update, and remove applications</a> from their devices. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Play_Store" title="Google Play Store">Google Play Store</a> is the primary application store installed on Android devices that comply with Google's compatibility requirements and license the Google Mobile Services software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geek-poweredby_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-geek-poweredby-78">[76]</a></sup> Google Play Store allows users to browse, download and update applications published by Google and third-party developers; as of July 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Android_(operating_system)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are more than one million applications available for Android in Play Store.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> As of July 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Android_(operating_system)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 50 billion applications have been installed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google_Play_Hits_1_Million_Apps_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Google_Play_Hits_1_Million_Apps-80">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-android-compatibility_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-android-compatibility-81">[79]</a></sup> Some carriers offer direct carrier billing for Google Play application purchases, where the cost of the application is added to the user's monthly bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to the open nature of Android, a number of third-party application marketplaces also exist for Android, either to provide a substitute for devices that are not allowed to ship with Google Play Store, provide applications that cannot be offered on Google Play Store due to policy violations, or for other reasons. Examples of these third-party stores have included the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Appstore" title="Amazon Appstore">Amazon Appstore</a>, <a href="/wiki/GetJar" title="GetJar">GetJar</a>, and SlideMe. <a href="/wiki/F-Droid" title="F-Droid">F-Droid</a>, another alternative marketplace, seeks to only provide applications that are distributed under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_and_open_source" title="Free and open source">free and open source</a> <a href="/wiki/Free_software_license" title="Free software license">licenses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired_alt_app_stores_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired_alt_app_stores-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-fossexperiment_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars-fossexperiment-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-adblockingevicted_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars-adblockingevicted-85">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memory_management">Memory management</span></h3>
<p>Since Android devices are usually battery-powered, Android is designed to manage processes to keep power consumption at a minimum. When an application is not in use the system <a href="/wiki/Process_state" title="Process state">suspends its operation</a> so that, while available for immediate use rather than closed, it does not use battery power or CPU resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phonedogtask_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-phonedogtask-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android manages the applications stored in memory automatically: when memory is low, the system will begin invisibly and automatically closing inactive processes, starting with those that have been inactive for longest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phandroidtask_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-phandroidtask-88">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> Lifehacker reported in 2011 that third-party task killers were doing more harm than good.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lifehackertask_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-lifehackertask-90">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virtual_reality">Virtual reality</span></h3>
<p>At <a href="/wiki/Google_I/O" title="Google I/O">Google I/O</a> on May 2016, Google announced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daydream_(Google)" title="Daydream (Google)">Daydream</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality" title="Virtual reality">virtual reality</a> platform that relies on a smartphone and provides VR capabilities through a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality_headset" title="Virtual reality headset">virtual reality headset</a> and controller designed by Google itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> The platform is built into Android starting with <a href="/wiki/Android_Nougat" title="Android Nougat">Android Nougat</a>, differentiating from standalone support for VR capabilities. The software is available for developers, and was released in 2016.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hardware"><span id="HWREQ"></span>Hardware</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HTC_Android_T-Mobile_G1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="345" data-file-width="500" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/HTC_Android_T-Mobile_G1.jpg/250px-HTC_Android_T-Mobile_G1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/HTC_Android_T-Mobile_G1.jpg/375px-HTC_Android_T-Mobile_G1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/HTC_Android_T-Mobile_G1.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HTC_Android_T-Mobile_G1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/HTC_Dream" title="HTC Dream">HTC Dream</a> or T-Mobile G1, the first commercially released device running Android (2008).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Android_hardware_requirements" title="Android hardware requirements">Android hardware requirements</a></div>
<p>The main hardware platform for Android is the <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" title="ARM architecture">ARM</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARMv7" title="ARMv7">ARMv7</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARMv8-A" title="ARMv8-A">ARMv8-A</a> architectures), with <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> and <a href="/wiki/MIPS_instruction_set" title="MIPS instruction set">MIPS</a> architectures also officially supported in later versions of Android. Since Android 5.0 "Lollipop", <a href="/wiki/64-bit_computing" title="64-bit computing">64-bit</a> variants of all platforms are supported in addition to the <a href="/wiki/32-bit" title="32-bit">32-bit</a> variants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> The unofficial <a href="/wiki/Android-x86" title="Android-x86">Android-x86</a> project provided support for the x86 architectures ahead of the official support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARMAN-4.0-on-x86_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-ARMAN-4.0-on-x86-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> MIPS architecture was also supported before Google did. Since 2012, Android devices with <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> processors began to appear, including phones<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> and tablets. While gaining support for 64-bit platforms, Android was first made to run on 64-bit x86 and then on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARM64" title="ARM64">ARM64</a>.</p>
<p>Requirements for the minimum amount of <a href="/wiki/Random-access_memory" title="Random-access memory">RAM</a> for devices running Android 5.1 range from 512 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MiB" title="MiB">MB</a> of RAM for normal-density screens, to about 1.8 GB for high-density screens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> The recommendation for Android 4.4 is to have at least 512 MB of RAM,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> while for "low RAM" devices 340 MB is the required minimum amount that does not include memory dedicated to various hardware components such as the <a href="/wiki/Baseband_processor" title="Baseband processor">baseband processor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> Android 4.4 requires a <a href="/wiki/32-bit" title="32-bit">32-bit</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARMv7" title="ARMv7">ARMv7</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIPS_architecture" title="MIPS architecture">MIPS</a> or <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> architecture processor (latter two through unofficial ports),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARMAN-4.0-on-x86_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-ARMAN-4.0-on-x86-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup> together with an <a href="/wiki/OpenGL_ES" title="OpenGL ES">OpenGL ES</a> 2.0 compatible <a href="/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit" title="Graphics processing unit">graphics processing unit</a> (GPU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-android-opengl-2.0_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-android-opengl-2.0-99">[97]</a></sup> Android supports OpenGL ES 1.1, 2.0, 3.0, 3.1 and as of latest major version, 3.2 and <a href="/wiki/Vulkan_(API)" title="Vulkan (API)">Vulkan</a>. Some applications may explicitly require a certain version of the OpenGL ES, and suitable GPU hardware is required to run such applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-android-opengl-2.0_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-android-opengl-2.0-99">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android devices incorporate many optional hardware components, including still or video cameras, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS" title="GPS">GPS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orientation_sensing" title="Orientation sensing">orientation sensors</a>, dedicated gaming controls, <a href="/wiki/Accelerometer" title="Accelerometer">accelerometers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a>, barometers, <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proximity_sensor" title="Proximity sensor">proximity sensors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pressure_sensor" title="Pressure sensor">pressure sensors</a>, thermometers, and <a href="/wiki/Touchscreen" title="Touchscreen">touchscreens</a>. Some hardware components are not required, but became standard in certain classes of devices, such as smartphones, and additional requirements apply if they are present. Some other hardware was initially required, but those requirements have been relaxed or eliminated altogether. For example, as Android was developed initially as a phone OS, hardware such as microphones were required, while over time the phone function became optional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-android-compatibility_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-android-compatibility-81">[79]</a></sup> Android used to require an <a href="/wiki/Autofocus" title="Autofocus">autofocus</a> camera, which was relaxed to a <a href="/wiki/Fixed-focus_lens" title="Fixed-focus lens">fixed-focus</a> camera<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-android-compatibility_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-android-compatibility-81">[79]</a></sup> if present at all, since the camera was dropped as a requirement entirely when Android started to be used on <a href="/wiki/Set-top_box" title="Set-top box">set-top boxes</a>.</p>
<p>In addition to running on smartphones and tablets, several vendors run Android natively on regular PC hardware with a keyboard and mouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> In addition to their availability on commercially available hardware, similar PC hardware-friendly versions of Android are freely available from the Android-x86 project, including customized Android 4.4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> Using the Android <a href="/wiki/Emulator" title="Emulator">emulator</a> that is part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Android_SDK" title="Android SDK">Android SDK</a>, or by using <a href="/wiki/BlueStacks" title="BlueStacks">BlueStacks</a> or Andy, Android can also run non-natively on x86.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> Chinese companies are building a PC and mobile operating system, based on Android, to "compete directly with Microsoft Windows and Google Android".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> The Chinese Academy of Engineering noted that "more than a dozen" companies were customising Android following a Chinese ban on the use of Windows 8 on government PCs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Android_green_figure,_next_to_its_original_packaging.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Android_green_figure%2C_next_to_its_original_packaging.jpg/220px-Android_green_figure%2C_next_to_its_original_packaging.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Android_green_figure%2C_next_to_its_original_packaging.jpg/330px-Android_green_figure%2C_next_to_its_original_packaging.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Android_green_figure%2C_next_to_its_original_packaging.jpg/440px-Android_green_figure%2C_next_to_its_original_packaging.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Android_green_figure,_next_to_its_original_packaging.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Android green figure, next to its original packaging</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Android is developed in private by Google until the latest changes and updates are ready to be released, at which point the source code is made available publicly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> This source code will only run without modification on select devices, usually the <a href="/wiki/Google_Nexus" title="Google Nexus">Nexus</a> series of devices. The source code is, in turn, adapted by <a href="/wiki/Original_equipment_manufacturer" title="Original equipment manufacturer">original equipment manufacturers</a> (OEMs) to run on their hardware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> Android's source code does not contain the often proprietary <a href="/wiki/Device_driver" title="Device driver">device drivers</a> that are needed for certain hardware components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-android1_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-android1-113">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, the green Android logo was designed for Google by graphic designer <a href="/wiki/Irina_Blok" title="Irina Blok">Irina Blok</a>. The design team was tasked with a project to create a universally identifiable icon with the specific inclusion of a robot in the final design. After numerous design developments based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science-fiction" title="Science-fiction">science-fiction</a> and space movies, the team eventually sought inspiration from the human symbol on restroom doors and modified the figure into a robot shape. As Android is open-sourced, it was agreed that the logo should be likewise, and since its launch the green logo has been reinterpreted into countless variations on the original design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-androidlogo4_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-androidlogo4-114">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Update_schedule">Update schedule</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Android_version_history" title="Android version history">Android version history</a></div>
<p>Google provides major incremental upgrades to Android every six to nine months, with <a href="/wiki/Confectionery" title="Confectionery">confectionery</a>-themed names, which most devices are capable of receiving <a href="/wiki/Over-the-air_programming" title="Over-the-air programming">over the air</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup> The latest major release is Android 7.0 "Nougat".</p>
<p>Compared to its primary rival mobile operating system, <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a>, Android updates typically reach various devices with significant delays. Except for devices with the <a href="/wiki/Google_Nexus" title="Google Nexus">Google Nexus</a> brand, updates often arrive months after the release of the new version, or not at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alliance_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-alliance-116">[114]</a></sup> This is partly due to the extensive variation in <a href="/wiki/Computer_hardware" title="Computer hardware">hardware</a> of Android devices, to which each upgrade must be specifically tailored, as the official Google source code only runs on their own Nexus devices. <a href="/wiki/Porting" title="Porting">Porting</a> Android to specific hardware is a time- and resource-consuming process for device manufacturers, who prioritize their newest devices and often leave older ones behind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alliance_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-alliance-116">[114]</a></sup> Hence, older smartphones are frequently not updated if the manufacturer decides it is not worth the investment of resources, although the device may be compatible. This problem is compounded when manufacturers customize Android with their own interface and apps, which must be reapplied to each new release. Additional delays can be introduced by wireless carriers who, after receiving updates from manufacturers, further customize and brand Android to their needs and conduct extensive testing on their networks before sending the upgrade out to users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alliance_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-alliance-116">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The lack of after-sale support from manufacturers and carriers has been widely criticized by consumer groups and the technology media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gu-updates_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-gu-updates-118">[116]</a></sup> Some commentators have noted that the industry has a financial incentive not to upgrade their devices, as the lack of updates for existing devices fuels the purchase of newer ones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup> an attitude described as "insulting".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gu-updates_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-gu-updates-118">[116]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> complained that the method of distribution for updates is complicated only because manufacturers and carriers have designed it that way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gu-updates_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-gu-updates-118">[116]</a></sup> In 2011, Google partnered with a number of industry players to announce an "Android Update Alliance", pledging to deliver timely updates for every device for 18 months after its release;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> however, there has not been another official word about that alliance since its announcement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alliance_116-3"><a href="#cite_note-alliance-116">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012, Google began decoupling certain aspects of the operating system (particularly core applications) so they could be updated through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Play_Store" title="Google Play Store">Google Play Store</a> independently of the operating system. One of these components, <a href="/wiki/Google_Play_Services" title="Google Play Services">Google Play Services</a>, is a closed-source system-level process providing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/API" title="API">APIs</a> for Google services, installed automatically on nearly all devices running Android version 2.2 and higher. With these changes, Google can add new operating system functionality through Play Services and application updates without having to distribute an upgrade to the operating system itself. As a result, Android 4.2 and 4.3 contained relatively fewer user-facing changes, focusing more on minor changes and platform improvements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-googleplayservices_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars-googleplayservices-122">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2016, it was announced that Google is considering "shaming" smartphone makers who fail to release updated versions of Android to their devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linux_kernel">Linux kernel</span></h3>
<p>Android's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_(computing)" title="Kernel (computing)">kernel</a> is based on one of the <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">Linux kernel</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Long-term_support" title="Long-term support">long-term support</a> (LTS) branches. Since April 2014, Android devices mainly use versions 3.4, 3.10 or 3.18 of the Linux kernel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup> The specific kernel version depends on the actual Android device and its hardware platform;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup> Android has used various kernel versions since the version 2.6.25 that was used in Android 1.0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kernels_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-kernels-44">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android's variant of the Linux kernel has further architectural changes that are implemented by Google outside the typical Linux kernel development cycle, such as the inclusion of components like <a href="/wiki/OpenBinder" title="OpenBinder">Binder</a>, ashmem, pmem, logger, wakelocks, and different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Out-of-memory" title="Out-of-memory">out-of-memory</a> (OOM) handling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> Certain features that Google contributed back to the Linux kernel, notably a power management feature called "wakelocks", were rejected by mainline kernel developers partly because they felt that Google did not show any intent to maintain its own code.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hartmann2_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-hartmann2-132">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hartmann_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-hartmann-133">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> Google announced in April 2010 that they would hire two employees to work with the Linux kernel community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Greg_Kroah-Hartman" title="Greg Kroah-Hartman">Greg Kroah-Hartman</a>, the current Linux kernel maintainer for the stable branch, said in December 2010 that he was concerned that Google was no longer trying to get their code changes included in mainstream Linux.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hartmann_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-hartmann-133">[131]</a></sup> Some Google Android developers hinted that "the Android team was getting fed up with the process," because they were a small team and had more urgent work to do on Android.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2011, <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">Linus Torvalds</a> said that "eventually Android and Linux would come back to a common kernel, but it will probably not be for four to five years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup> In December 2011, Greg Kroah-Hartman announced the start of Android Mainlining Project, which aims to put some Android <a href="/wiki/Device_driver" title="Device driver">drivers</a>, patches and features back into the Linux kernel, starting in Linux 3.3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> Linux included the autosleep and wakelocks capabilities in the 3.5 kernel, after many previous attempts at merger. The interfaces are the same but the upstream Linux implementation allows for two different suspend modes: to memory (the traditional suspend that Android uses), and to disk (hibernate, as it is known on the desktop).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> Google maintains a public code repository that contains their experimental work to <a href="/wiki/Rebasing" title="Rebasing">re-base</a> Android off the latest stable Linux versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flash_storage" title="Flash storage">flash storage</a> on Android devices is split into several partitions, such as <code>/system</code> for the operating system itself, and <code>/data</code> for user data and application installations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup> In contrast to desktop Linux distributions, Android device owners are not given <a href="/wiki/Superuser" title="Superuser">root</a> access to the operating system and sensitive partitions such as /system are <a href="/wiki/Read-only_memory" title="Read-only memory">read-only</a>. However, root access can be obtained by exploiting <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_(computing)" title="Vulnerability (computing)">security flaws</a> in Android, which is used frequently by the <a href="#Open_source_community">open-source community</a> to enhance the capabilities of their devices,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup> but also by malicious parties to install <a href="/wiki/Computer_virus" title="Computer virus">viruses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android is a <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">Linux distribution</a> according to the <a href="/wiki/Linux_Foundation" title="Linux Foundation">Linux Foundation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup> Google's open-source chief <a href="/wiki/Chris_DiBona" title="Chris DiBona">Chris DiBona</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup> and several journalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup> Others, such as Google engineer Patrick Brady, say that Android is not Linux in the traditional <a href="/wiki/Unix-like" title="Unix-like">Unix-like</a> Linux distribution sense; Android does not include the <a href="/wiki/GNU_C_Library" title="GNU C Library">GNU C Library</a> (it uses <a href="/wiki/Bionic_(software)" title="Bionic (software)">Bionic</a> as an alternative C library) and some of other components typically found in Linux distributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APIs_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-APIs-149">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Software_stack">Software stack</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Android-System-Architecture.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="735" data-file-width="906" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Android-System-Architecture.svg/350px-Android-System-Architecture.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Android-System-Architecture.svg/525px-Android-System-Architecture.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Android-System-Architecture.svg/700px-Android-System-Architecture.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Android-System-Architecture.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Android's architecture diagram</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On top of the Linux kernel, there are the <a href="/wiki/Middleware" title="Middleware">middleware</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Software_library" title="Software library">libraries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">APIs</a> written in <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Application_software" title="Application software">application software</a> running on an <a href="/wiki/Application_framework" title="Application framework">application framework</a> which includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oracle_Java" title="Oracle Java">Java</a>-compatible libraries. Development of the Linux kernel continues independently of other Android's source code bases.</p>
<p>Until version 5.0, Android used <a href="/wiki/Dalvik_(software)" title="Dalvik (software)">Dalvik</a> as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Process_virtual_machine" title="Process virtual machine">process virtual machine</a> with <a href="/wiki/Tracing_just-in-time_compilation" title="Tracing just-in-time compilation">trace-based just-in-time (JIT) compilation</a> to run Dalvik "dex-code" (Dalvik Executable), which is usually translated from the <a href="/wiki/Java_bytecode" title="Java bytecode">Java bytecode</a>. Following the trace-based JIT principle, in addition to <a href="/wiki/Interpreter_(computing)" title="Interpreter (computing)">interpreting</a> the majority of application code, Dalvik performs the compilation and <a href="/wiki/Machine_code" title="Machine code">native execution</a> of select frequently executed code segments ("traces") each time an application is launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brady_dissects_android_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-brady_dissects_android-152">[150]</a></sup> Android 4.4 introduced <a href="/wiki/Android_Runtime" title="Android Runtime">Android Runtime</a> (ART) as a new runtime environment, which uses <a href="/wiki/Ahead-of-time_compilation" title="Ahead-of-time compilation">ahead-of-time (AOT) compilation</a> to entirely compile the application bytecode into <a href="/wiki/Machine_code" title="Machine code">machine code</a> upon the installation of an application. In Android 4.4, ART was an experimental feature and not enabled by default; it became the only runtime option in the next major version of Android, 5.0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>For its Java library, the Android platform uses a subset of the now discontinued <a href="/wiki/Apache_Harmony" title="Apache Harmony">Apache Harmony</a> project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-njdk_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars-njdk-154">[152]</a></sup> In December 2015, Google announced that the next version of Android would switch to a Java implementation based on <a href="/wiki/OpenJDK" title="OpenJDK">OpenJDK</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vb-openjdk_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-vb-openjdk-155">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android's <a href="/wiki/C_standard_library" title="C standard library">standard C library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bionic_(software)" title="Bionic (software)">Bionic</a>, was developed by Google specifically for Android, as a derivation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BSD" title="BSD">BSD</a>'s standard C library code. Bionic itself has been designed with several major features specific to the Linux kernel. The main benefits of using Bionic instead of the <a href="/wiki/GNU_C_Library" title="GNU C Library">GNU C Library</a> (glibc) or <a href="/wiki/UClibc" title="UClibc">uClibc</a> are its smaller runtime footprint, and optimization for low-frequency CPUs. At the same time, Bionic is licensed under the terms of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BSD_licence" title="BSD licence">BSD licence</a>, which Google finds more suitable for the Android's overall licensing model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brady_dissects_android_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-brady_dissects_android-152">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aiming for a different licensing model, toward the end of 2012, Google switched the Bluetooth stack in Android from the GPL-licensed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BlueZ" title="BlueZ">BlueZ</a> to the Apache-licensed BlueDroid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android does not have a native <a href="/wiki/X_Window_System" title="X Window System">X Window System</a> by default, nor does it support the full set of standard <a href="/wiki/GNU" title="GNU">GNU</a> libraries. This made it difficult to port existing Linux applications or libraries to Android,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APIs_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-APIs-149">[147]</a></sup> until version r5 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Android_Native_Development_Kit" title="Android Native Development Kit">Android Native Development Kit</a> brought support for applications written completely in <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a> or <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup> Libraries written in C may also be used in applications by injection of a small <a href="/wiki/Shim_(computing)" title="Shim (computing)">shim</a> and usage of the <a href="/wiki/Java_Native_Interface" title="Java Native Interface">JNI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdlandroid_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdlandroid-158">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since Marshmallow, "<a href="/wiki/Toybox" title="Toybox">Toybox</a>", a collection of command line utilities (mostly for use by apps, as Android doesn't provide a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Command_line_interface" title="Command line interface">command line interface</a> by default), replaced similar "Toolbox" collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toyboxmarsmallow_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-toyboxmarsmallow-159">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android has another operating system, Trusty OS, within it, as a part of "Trusty" "software components supporting a Trusted Execution Environment (TEE) on mobile devices." "Trusty and the Trusty API are subject to change. [..] Applications for the Trusty OS can be written in C/C++ (C++ support is limited), and they have access to a small C library. [..] All Trusty applications are single-threaded; multithreading in Trusty userspace currently is unsupported. [..] Third-party application development is not supported in" the current version, and software running on the OS and processor for it, run the "<a href="/wiki/Digital_rights_management" title="Digital rights management">DRM</a> framework for protected content. [..] There are many other uses for a TEE such as mobile payments, secure banking, full-disk encryption, multi-factor authentication, device reset protection, replay-protected persistent storage, wireless display ("cast") of protected content, secure PIN and fingerprint processing, and even malware detection."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Open-source_community"><span id="AOSP"></span>Open-source community</span></h3>
<p>Android has an active community of developers and enthusiasts who use the <i>Android Open Source Project</i> (AOSP) source code to develop and distribute their own modified versions of the operating system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnetroms_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnetroms-161">[159]</a></sup> These community-developed releases often bring new features and updates to devices faster than through the official manufacturer/carrier channels, with a comparable level of quality;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apolroms_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-apolroms-28">[26]</a></sup> provide continued support for older devices that no longer receive official updates; or bring Android to devices that were officially released running other operating systems, such as the <a href="/wiki/HP_TouchPad" title="HP TouchPad">HP TouchPad</a>. Community releases often come pre-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Android_rooting" title="Android rooting">rooted</a> and contain modifications not provided by the original vendor, such as the ability to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overclock" title="Overclock">overclock</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_voltage_scaling" title="Dynamic voltage scaling">over/undervolt</a> the device's processor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/CyanogenMod" title="CyanogenMod">CyanogenMod</a> was the most widely used community firmware,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup> and CyanogenMod has been discontinued and <a href="/wiki/LineageOS" title="LineageOS">LineageOS</a> is the successor of CyanogenMod<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup> l <a href="/wiki/Android-x86" title="Android-x86">Android-x86</a> is a version of Android for <a href="/wiki/IBM_PC_compatible" title="IBM PC compatible">IBM PC compatibles</a>. There have also been attempts with varying degrees of success to port Android to iPhones, notably the iDroid Project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, device manufacturers and mobile carriers have typically been unsupportive of third-party <a href="/wiki/Firmware" title="Firmware">firmware</a> development. Manufacturers express concern about improper functioning of devices running unofficial software and the support costs resulting from this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-htcdev_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-htcdev-166">[164]</a></sup> Moreover, modified firmwares such as CyanogenMod sometimes offer features, such as <a href="/wiki/Tethering" title="Tethering">tethering</a>, for which carriers would otherwise charge a premium. As a result, technical obstacles including locked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bootloader" title="Bootloader">bootloaders</a> and restricted access to root permissions are common in many devices. However, as community-developed software has grown more popular, and following a statement by the Librarian of Congress in the United States that permits the "<a href="/wiki/IOS_jailbreaking" title="IOS jailbreaking">jailbreaking</a>" of mobile devices,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup> manufacturers and carriers have softened their position regarding third party development, with some, including <a href="/wiki/HTC" title="HTC">HTC</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-htcdev_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-htcdev-166">[164]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Motorola" title="Motorola">Motorola</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sony_Mobile_Communications" title="Sony Mobile Communications">Sony</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup> providing support and encouraging development. As a result of this, over time the need to circumvent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardware_restrictions" title="Hardware restrictions">hardware restrictions</a> to install unofficial firmware has lessened as an increasing number of devices are shipped with unlocked or unlockable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bootloader" title="Bootloader">bootloaders</a>, similar to <a href="/wiki/Google_Nexus" title="Google Nexus">Nexus</a> series of phones, although usually requiring that users waive their devices' warranties to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-htcdev_166-2"><a href="#cite_note-htcdev-166">[164]</a></sup> However, despite manufacturer acceptance, some carriers in the US still require that phones are locked down, frustrating developers and customers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-c_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-c-172">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_and_privacy"><span id="APP-OPS"></span>Security and privacy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mobile_security" title="Mobile security">Mobile security</a> and <a href="/wiki/WARRIOR_PRIDE" title="WARRIOR PRIDE">WARRIOR PRIDE</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scope_of_surveillance_by_public_institutions">Scope of surveillance by public institutions</span></h3>
<p>As part of the broader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013_mass_surveillance_disclosures" title="2013 mass surveillance disclosures">2013 mass surveillance disclosures</a> it was revealed in September 2013 that the American and British intelligence agencies, the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> (NSA) and <a href="/wiki/Government_Communications_Headquarters" title="Government Communications Headquarters">Government Communications Headquarters</a> (GCHQ), respectively, have access to the user data on iPhone, BlackBerry, and Android devices. They are reportedly able to read almost all smartphone information, including SMS, location, emails, and notes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spiegel20130907_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-spiegel20130907-173">[171]</a></sup> In January 2014, further reports revealed the intelligence agencies' capabilities to intercept the personal information transmitted across the Internet by social networks and other popular applications such as <a href="/wiki/Angry_Birds" title="Angry Birds">Angry Birds</a>, which collect personal information of their users for advertising and other commercial reasons. GCHQ has, according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Wiki" title="Wiki">wiki</a>-style guide of different apps and advertising networks, and the different data that can be siphoned from each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-angrybirdsatgchq_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-angrybirdsatgchq-174">[172]</a></sup> Later that week, the Finnish Angry Birds developer <a href="/wiki/Rovio_Entertainment" title="Rovio Entertainment">Rovio</a> announced that it was reconsidering its relationships with its advertising platforms in the light of these revelations, and called upon the wider industry to do the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>The documents revealed a further effort by the intelligence agencies to intercept Google Maps searches and queries submitted from Android and other smartphones to collect location information in bulk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-angrybirdsatgchq_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-angrybirdsatgchq-174">[172]</a></sup> The NSA and GCHQ insist their activities are in compliance with all relevant domestic and international laws, although the Guardian stated "the latest disclosures could also add to mounting public concern about how the technology sector collects and uses information, especially for those outside the US, who enjoy fewer privacy protections than Americans."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-angrybirdsatgchq_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-angrybirdsatgchq-174">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_security_threats">Common security threats</span></h3>
<p>Research from security company <a href="/wiki/Trend_Micro" title="Trend Micro">Trend Micro</a> lists premium service abuse as the most common type of Android malware, where text messages are sent from infected phones to <a href="/wiki/Premium-rate_telephone_number" title="Premium-rate telephone number">premium-rate telephone numbers</a> without the consent or even knowledge of the user.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-micro_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-micro-176">[174]</a></sup> Other malware displays unwanted and intrusive advertisements on the device, or sends personal information to unauthorised third parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-micro_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-micro-176">[174]</a></sup> Security threats on Android are reportedly growing exponentially; however, Google engineers have argued that the malware and virus threat on Android is being <a href="/wiki/Fear,_uncertainty_and_doubt" title="Fear, uncertainty and doubt">exaggerated</a> by security companies for commercial reasons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exaggeration_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-exaggeration-177">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extremely_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-extremely-178">[176]</a></sup> and have accused the security industry of playing on fears to sell virus protection software to users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exaggeration_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-exaggeration-177">[175]</a></sup> Google maintains that dangerous malware is actually extremely rare,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extremely_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-extremely-178">[176]</a></sup> and a survey conducted by <a href="/wiki/F-Secure" title="F-Secure">F-Secure</a> showed that only 0.5% of Android malware reported had come from the Google Play store.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup></p>
