<p>Android's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platform_fragmentation" title="Platform fragmentation">fragmentation</a> is a problem for security, since patches to bugs found in the core operating system often do not reach users of older and lower-price devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodbye.2C_Android_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodbye.2C_Android-180">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup> One set of researchers say that the failure of vendors to support older devices with patches and updates leaves more than 87% of active devices vulnerable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup> However, the open-source nature of Android allows security contractors to take existing devices and adapt them for highly secure uses. For example, Samsung has worked with General Dynamics through their <a href="/wiki/Open_Kernel_Labs" title="Open Kernel Labs">Open Kernel Labs</a> acquisition to rebuild <i>Jelly Bean</i> on top of their hardened microvisor for the "Knox" project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android smartphones have the ability to report the location of <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> access points, encountered as phone users move around, to build databases containing the physical locations of hundreds of millions of such access points. These databases form electronic maps to locate smartphones, allowing them to run apps like <a href="/wiki/Foursquare" title="Foursquare">Foursquare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_Latitude" title="Google Latitude">Google Latitude</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Facebook_Places" title="Facebook Places">Facebook Places</a>, and to deliver location-based ads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup> Third party monitoring software such as TaintDroid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[185]</a></sup> an academic research-funded project, can, in some cases, detect when personal information is being sent from applications to remote servers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_security_features">Technical security features</span></h3>
<p>Android applications run in a <a href="/wiki/Sandbox_(computer_security)" title="Sandbox (computer security)">sandbox</a>, an isolated area of the system that does not have access to the rest of the system's resources, unless access permissions are explicitly granted by the user when the application is installed. Before installing an application, <a href="/wiki/Google_Play" title="Google Play">Play Store</a> displays all required permissions: a game may need to enable vibration or save data to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SD_card" title="SD card">SD card</a>, for example, but should not need to read SMS messages or access the phonebook. After reviewing these permissions, the user can choose to accept or refuse them, installing the application only if they accept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[187]</a></sup> The sandboxing and permissions system lessens the impact of vulnerabilities and bugs in applications, but developer confusion and limited documentation has resulted in applications routinely requesting unnecessary permissions, reducing its effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demystified_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-demystified-190">[188]</a></sup> Google has now pushed an update to Android Verify Apps feature, which will now run in background to detect malicious processes and crack them down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Android 6.0 <i>Marshmallow</i>, the permissions system was changed to allow the user to control an application's permissions individually, to block applications if desired from having access to the device's contacts, calendar, phone, sensors, SMS, location, microphone and camera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup> Full permission control is only possible with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Android_rooting" title="Android rooting">root</a> access to the device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Google uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Bouncer" title="Google Bouncer">Google Bouncer</a> malware scanner to watch over and scan applications available in the Google Play Store.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup> It is intended to flag suspicious apps and warn users of any potential threat with an application before they download it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup> Android version 4.2 <i>Jelly Bean</i> was released in 2012, with enhanced security features, including a malware scanner built into the system, which works in combination with Google Play but can scan apps installed from third party sources as well, and an alert system which notifies the user when an app tries to send a premium-rate text message, blocking the message unless the user explicitly authorises it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup> Several security firms, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lookout_Mobile_Security" title="Lookout Mobile Security">Lookout Mobile Security</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[195]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/AVG_Technologies" title="AVG Technologies">AVG Technologies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McAfee" title="McAfee">McAfee</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[197]</a></sup> have released antivirus software for Android devices. This software is ineffective as sandboxing also applies to such applications, limiting their ability to scan the deeper system for threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extremetech_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-extremetech-200">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[199]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2013, Google released Android Device Manager (ADM), a component that allows users to remotely track, locate, and wipe their Android device through a web interface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-googleplayservices_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-ars-googleplayservices-122">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-adm_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-adm-202">[200]</a></sup> In December 2013, Google released ADM as an Android application on the Google Play store, where it is available to devices running Android version 2.2 and higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Licensing">Licensing</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Source_code" title="Source code">source code</a> for Android is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open-source" title="Open-source">open-source</a>: it is developed in private by Google, with the source code released publicly when a new version of Android is released. Google publishes most of the code (including network and telephony <a href="/wiki/Solution_stack" title="Solution stack">stacks</a>) under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-copyleft" title="Non-copyleft">non-copyleft</a> <a href="/wiki/Apache_License" title="Apache License">Apache License</a> version 2.0. which allows modification and redistribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup> The license does not grant rights to the "Android" trademark, so device manufacturers and wireless carriers have to license it from Google under individual contracts. Associated Linux kernel changes are released under the <a href="/wiki/Copyleft" title="Copyleft">copyleft</a> <a href="/wiki/GNU_General_Public_License" title="GNU General Public License">GNU General Public License</a> version 2, developed by the <a href="/wiki/Open_Handset_Alliance" title="Open Handset Alliance">Open Handset Alliance</a>, with the source code publicly available at all times. Typically, Google collaborates with a hardware manufacturer to produce a flagship device (part of the Nexus series) featuring the new version of Android, then makes the source code available after that device has been released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup> The only Android release which was not immediately made available as source code was the tablet-only 3.0 <i>Honeycomb</i> release. The reason, according to <a href="/wiki/Andy_Rubin" title="Andy Rubin">Andy Rubin</a> in an official Android blog post, was because <i>Honeycomb</i> was rushed for production of the <a href="/wiki/Motorola_Xoom" title="Motorola Xoom">Motorola Xoom</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup> and they did not want third parties creating a "really bad user experience" by attempting to put onto smartphones a version of Android intended for tablets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>Only the base Android operating system (including some applications) is open-source software, whereas most Android devices ship with a substantial amount of proprietary software, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Mobile_Services" title="Google Mobile Services">Google Mobile Services</a>, which includes applications such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Play_Store" title="Google Play Store">Google Play Store</a>, Google Search, and <a href="/wiki/Google_Play_Services" title="Google Play Services">Google Play Services</a> –  a software layer that provides <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">APIs</a> for the integration with Google-provided services, among others. These applications must be licensed from Google by device makers, and can only be shipped on devices which meet its compatibility guidelines and other requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geek-poweredby_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-geek-poweredby-78">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-googleplayservices_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-ars-googleplayservices-122">[120]</a></sup> Custom, certified distributions of Android produced by manufacturers (such as <a href="/wiki/TouchWiz" title="TouchWiz">TouchWiz</a> and <a href="/wiki/HTC_Sense" title="HTC Sense">HTC Sense</a>) may also replace certain stock Android apps with their own proprietary variants and add additional software not included in the stock Android operating system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup> There may also be "<a href="/wiki/Binary_blob" title="Binary blob">binary blob</a>" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Driver_(software)" title="Driver (software)">drivers</a> required for certain hardware components in the device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-android1_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-android1-113">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some stock applications in AOSP code that were formerly used by earlier versions of Android, such as Search, Music, and Calendar, have been <a href="/wiki/Deprecation" title="Deprecation">abandoned</a> by Google in favor of <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_software" title="Proprietary software">non-free</a> replacements distributed through Play Store (Google Search, Google Play Music, and Google Calendar) that are no longer open-source. Moreover, open-source variants of some applications also exclude functions that are present in their non-free versions, such as Photosphere panoramas in Camera, and a <a href="/wiki/Google_Now" title="Google Now">Google Now</a> page on the default home screen (exclusive to the proprietary version "Google Now Launcher", whose code is embedded within that of the main Google application).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-n5launcher_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars-n5launcher-212">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Stallman" title="Richard Stallman">Richard Stallman</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Free_Software_Foundation" title="Free Software Foundation">Free Software Foundation</a> have been critical of Android and have recommended the usage of alternatives such as <a href="/wiki/Replicant_(operating_system)" title="Replicant (operating system)">Replicant</a>, because drivers and firmware vital for the proper functioning of Android devices are usually proprietary, and because the Google Play Store application can forcibly install or deinstall applications and, as a result, invite non-free software. although the Free Software Foundation has not found Google to use it for malicious reasons <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stallman2011_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-stallman2011-213">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leverage_over_manufacturers">Leverage over manufacturers</span></h3>
<p>Google licenses their Google Mobile Services software, along with Android trademarks, only to hardware manufacturers for devices that meet Google's compatibility standards specified in the Android Compatibility Program document.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndroidCompatibility_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-AndroidCompatibility-215">[213]</a></sup> Thus, forks of Android that make major changes to the operating system itself do not include any of Google's non-free components, stay incompatible with applications that require them, and must ship with an alternative software marketplace in lieu of Google Play Store.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup> Examples of such Android forks are <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fire_OS" title="Fire OS">Fire OS</a> (which is used on the <a href="/wiki/Kindle_Fire" title="Kindle Fire">Kindle Fire</a> line of tablets, and oriented toward Amazon services), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nokia_X_Software_Platform" title="Nokia X Software Platform">Nokia X Software Platform</a> (a fork used by the <a href="/wiki/Nokia_X_family" title="Nokia X family">Nokia X family</a>, oriented primarily toward <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> services), and other forks that exclude Google apps due to the general unavailability of Google services in certain regions (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_censorship_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Internet censorship in the People's Republic of China">China</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-nx_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-nx-216">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[215]</a></sup> In 2014, Google also began to require that all Android devices which license the Google Mobile Services software display a prominent "Powered by Android" logo on their boot screens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geek-poweredby_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-geek-poweredby-78">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Members of the Open Handset Alliance, which include the majority of Android OEMs, are also contractually forbidden from producing Android devices based on forks of the OS;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Compatibility_FAQ_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Compatibility_FAQ-218">[216]</a></sup> in 2012, <a href="/wiki/Acer_Inc." title="Acer Inc.">Acer Inc.</a> was forced by Google to halt production on a device powered by <a href="/wiki/Alibaba_Group" title="Alibaba Group">Alibaba Group</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aliyun_OS" title="Aliyun OS">Aliyun OS</a> with threats of removal from the OHA, as Google deemed the platform to be an incompatible version of Android. Alibaba Group defended the allegations, arguing that the OS was a distinct platform from Android (primarily using <a href="/wiki/HTML5" title="HTML5">HTML5</a> apps), but incorporated portions of Android's platform to allow backwards compatibility with third-party Android software. Indeed, the devices did ship with an application store which offered Android apps; however, the majority of them were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Software_piracy" title="Software piracy">pirated</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnet-alibabawrong_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnet-alibabawrong-219">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arstechnica-2012-09-17_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-arstechnica-2012-09-17-221">[219]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Android_x86_on_EeePC_701_4G.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Android_x86_on_EeePC_701_4G.jpg/220px-Android_x86_on_EeePC_701_4G.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Android_x86_on_EeePC_701_4G.jpg/330px-Android_x86_on_EeePC_701_4G.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Android_x86_on_EeePC_701_4G.jpg/440px-Android_x86_on_EeePC_701_4G.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Android_x86_on_EeePC_701_4G.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Android-x86" title="Android-x86">Android-x86</a> running on an ASUS <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EeePC" title="EeePC">EeePC</a> netbook; Android has been unofficially ported to traditional PCs for use as a desktop operating system.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Android received a lukewarm reaction when it was unveiled in 2007. Although analysts were impressed with the respected technology companies that had partnered with Google to form the Open Handset Alliance, it was unclear whether mobile phone manufacturers would be willing to replace their existing operating systems with Android.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup> The idea of an open-source, Linux-based <a href="/wiki/Computing_platform" title="Computing platform">development platform</a> sparked interest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup> but there were additional worries about Android facing strong competition from established players in the smartphone market, such as Nokia and Microsoft, and rival Linux mobile operating systems that were in development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[222]</a></sup> These established players were skeptical: <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a> was quoted as saying "we don't see this as a threat,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lol_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-lol-225">[223]</a></sup> and a member of Microsoft's Windows Mobile team stated "I don't understand the impact that they are going to have."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lol_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-lol-225">[223]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since then Android has grown to become the most widely used smartphone operating system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars5th_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-ars5th-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reisinger2015_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-reisinger2015-226">[224]</a></sup> and "one of the fastest mobile experiences available."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-syrup_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-syrup-227">[225]</a></sup> Reviewers have highlighted the open-source nature of the operating system as one of its defining strengths, allowing companies such as <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nokia_X_family" title="Nokia X family">Nokia X family</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kindle_Fire" title="Kindle Fire">Kindle Fire</a>), <a href="/wiki/Barnes_%26_Noble" title="Barnes &amp; Noble">Barnes &amp; Noble</a> (<a href="/wiki/Barnes_%26_Noble_Nook" title="Barnes &amp; Noble Nook">Nook</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ouya" title="Ouya">Ouya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baidu" title="Baidu">Baidu</a> and others to <a href="/wiki/Fork_(software_development)" title="Fork (software development)">fork</a> the software and release hardware running their own customised version of Android. As a result, it has been described by technology website <a href="/wiki/Ars_Technica" title="Ars Technica">Ars Technica</a> as "practically the default operating system for launching new hardware" for companies without their own mobile platforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars5th_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-ars5th-27">[25]</a></sup> This openness and flexibility is also present at the level of the end user: Android allows extensive customisation of devices by their owners and apps are freely available from non-Google app stores and third party websites. These have been cited as among the main advantages of Android phones over others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars5th_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-ars5th-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite Android's popularity, including an activation rate three times that of iOS, there have been reports that Google has not been able to leverage their other products and web services successfully to turn Android into the money maker that analysts had expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Verge" title="The Verge">The Verge</a> suggested that Google is losing control of Android due to the extensive customization and proliferation of non-Google apps and services –  Amazon's Kindle Fire line uses <a href="/wiki/Fire_OS" title="Fire OS">Fire OS</a>, a heavily modified fork of Android which does not include or support any of Google's proprietary components, and requires that users obtain software from its competing <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Appstore" title="Amazon Appstore">Amazon Appstore</a> instead of Play Store.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-irongrip_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-ars-irongrip-3">[3]</a></sup> In 2014, in an effort to improve prominence of the Android brand, Google began to require that devices featuring its proprietary components display an Android logo on the boot screen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geek-poweredby_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-geek-poweredby-78">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android has suffered from "fragmentation",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup> a situation where the variety of Android devices, in terms of both hardware variations and differences in the software running on them, makes the task of developing applications that work consistently across the ecosystem harder than rival platforms such as iOS where hardware and software varies less. For example, according to data from <a href="/wiki/OpenSignal" title="OpenSignal">OpenSignal</a> in July 2013, there were 11,868 models of Android device, numerous different screen sizes and eight Android OS versions simultaneously in use, while the large majority of iOS users have upgraded to the latest iteration of that OS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardianfrag_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardianfrag-232">[230]</a></sup> Critics such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple_Insider" title="Apple Insider">Apple Insider</a> have asserted that fragmentation via hardware and software pushed Android's growth through large volumes of low end, budget-priced devices running older versions of Android. They maintain this forces Android developers to write for the "lowest common denominator" to reach as many users as possible, who have too little incentive to make use of the latest hardware or software features only available on a smaller percentage of devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[231]</a></sup> However, OpenSignal, who develops both Android and iOS apps, concluded that although fragmentation can make development trickier, Android's wider global reach also increases the potential reward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardianfrag_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardianfrag-232">[230]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_share">Market share</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Usage_share_of_operating_systems" title="Usage share of operating systems">Usage share of operating systems</a></div>
<p>Research company Canalys estimated in the second quarter of 2009, that Android had a 2.8% share of worldwide <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphone</a> shipments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Insider_1_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Insider_1-234">[232]</a></sup> By the fourth quarter of 2010, this had grown to 33% of the market becoming the top-selling smartphone platform,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canalysQ42010_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-canalysQ42010-235">[233]</a></sup> overtaking <a href="/wiki/Symbian" title="Symbian">Symbian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[234]</a></sup> By the third quarter of 2011, <a href="/wiki/Gartner" title="Gartner">Gartner</a> estimated that more than half (52.5%) of the smartphone sales belonged to Android.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[235]</a></sup> By the third quarter of 2012 Android had a 75% share of the global smartphone market according to the research firm IDC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idcQ3_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-idcQ3-238">[236]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2011, Google said that 550,000 Android devices were being activated every day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-a550_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-a550-239">[237]</a></sup> up from 400,000 per day in May,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[238]</a></sup> and more than 100 million devices had been activated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-i.2Fo_2011_stats_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-i.2Fo_2011_stats-241">[239]</a></sup> with 4.4% growth per week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-a550_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-a550-239">[237]</a></sup> In September 2012, 500 million devices had been activated with 1.3 million activations per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google_Team_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-Google_Team-242">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Schmidt_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Schmidt-243">[241]</a></sup> In May 2013, at <a href="/wiki/Google_I/O" title="Google I/O">Google I/O</a>, Sundar Pichai announced that 900 million Android devices had been activated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-900_million_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-900_million-244">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android market share varies by location. In July 2012, "mobile subscribers aged 13+" in the United States using Android were up to 52%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[243]</a></sup> and rose to 90% in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[244]</a></sup> During the third quarter of 2012, Android's worldwide smartphone shipment market share was 75%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idcQ3_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-idcQ3-238">[236]</a></sup> with 750 million devices activated in total. In April 2013 Android had 1.5 million activations per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eric_Schmidt_243-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eric_Schmidt-243">[241]</a></sup> As of May 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Android_(operating_system)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 48 billion applications ("apps") have been installed from the Google Play store,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48bn_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-48bn-247">[245]</a></sup> and by September 2013, one billion Android devices have been activated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1bn_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-1bn-248">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of July 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Android_(operating_system)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Google_Play" title="Google Play">Google Play</a> store has had over one million Android applications published, and over 50 billion applications downloaded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phonearena1_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-phonearena1-23">[21]</a></sup> A developer survey conducted in April–May 2013 found that Android is used by 71% of mobile developers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-visionmobile1_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-visionmobile1-24">[22]</a></sup> The operating system's success has made it a target for patent litigation as part of the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Smartphone_patent_wars" title="Smartphone patent wars">smartphone wars</a>" between technology companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnet2011_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-cnet2011-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tomsguide_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-tomsguide-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Android devices account for more than half of smartphone sales in most markets, including the US, while "only in Japan was Apple on top" (September–November 2013 numbers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halfUS_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-halfUS-249">[247]</a></sup> At the end of 2013, over 1.5 billion Android smartphones have been sold in the four years since 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[249]</a></sup> making Android the most sold phone and tablet OS. Three billion Android smartphones are estimated to be sold by the end of 2014 (including previous years). According to Gartner research company, Android-based devices outsold all contenders, every year since 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup> In 2013, it outsold Windows 2.8:1 or by 573 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-more_than_Windows_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-more_than_Windows-253">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Android_(operating_system)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Android has the largest <a href="/wiki/Installed_base" title="Installed base">installed base</a> of all operating systems;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manjoomurky_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-manjoomurky-17">[15]</a></sup> Since 2013, devices running it also sell more than Windows, iOS and Mac OS X devices combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-best_selling_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-best_selling-256">[254]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/StatCounter" title="StatCounter">StatCounter</a>, which tracks only the use for browsing the web, Android is the most popular mobile operating system since August 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup> Android is the most popular operating system for web browsing in India and several other countries (e.g. virtually all of Asia, with Japan and North Korea exceptions). According to StatCounter, Android is most used on mobile in all African countries, and it stated "mobile usage has already overtaken desktop in several countries including India, South Africa and Saudi Arabia",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[256]</a></sup> with virtually all countries in Africa having done so already (except for seven countries, including Egypt), such as Ethiopia and Kenya in which mobile (including tablets) usage is at 90.46% (Android only, accounts for 75.81% of all use there<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[257]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Android phones in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a> commonly include Google's proprietary add-ons (such as Google Play) to the otherwise open-source operating system, this is increasingly not the case in emerging markets; "ABI Research claims that 65 million devices shipped globally with open-source Android in the second quarter of [2014], up from 54 million in the first quarter"; depending on country, percent of phones estimated to be based only on Android's source code (AOSP), forgoing the Android trademark: Thailand (44%), Philippines (38%), Indonesia (31%), India (21%), Malaysia (24%), Mexico (18%), Brazil (9%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a January 2015 <a href="/wiki/Gartner" title="Gartner">Gartner</a> report, "Android surpassed a billion shipments of devices in 2014, and will continue to grow at a double-digit pace in 2015, with a 26 percent increase year over year." This made it the first time that any general-purpose operating system has reached more than one billion end users within a year: by reaching close to 1.16 billion end users in 2014, Android shipped over four times more than <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OS_X" title="OS X">OS X</a> combined, and over three times more than <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a>. Gartner expected the whole mobile phone market to "reach two billion units in 2016", including Android.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[260]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a <a href="/wiki/Statistica" title="Statistica">Statistica</a>'s estimate, Android smartphones had an installed base of 1.8 billion units in 2015, which was 76% of the estimated total number of smartphones worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[c]</a></sup> Android has the largest installed base of any <a href="/wiki/Mobile_operating_system" title="Mobile operating system">mobile operating system</a> and, since 2013, the highest-selling operating system overall<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-more_than_Windows_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-more_than_Windows-253">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-best_selling_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-best_selling-256">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mahapatra1_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-mahapatra1-267">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elmer1_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-elmer1-268">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sammobile1_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-sammobile1-269">[266]</a></sup> with sales in 2012, 2013 and 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[267]</a></sup> close to the installed base of all PCs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[268]</a></sup> In the third quarter of 2015, Android's share of the global smartphone shipment market was 84.7%, the highest ever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[269]</a></sup> As of September 28, 2016, with 52.5% market share, Samsung remains the leading OEM for shipping Android running smartphoens and tablets, followed by followed by LG, Huawei, Motorola, Lenovo, Sony, HTC, Asus, Alcatel and Xiaomi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[270]</a></sup></p>
<p>By August 2016, the two biggest continents have gone mobile-majority, judged by web use ("desktop" has 46.92%–55.16% use worldwide, depending on day of the week, making some weeks desktop-minority;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[271]</a></sup> lowest full month was at 50.05%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[272]</a></sup>); because of Android (see <a href="/wiki/Usage_share_of_operating_systems#Crossover_to_mobile_getting_more_popular" title="Usage share of operating systems">usage share of operating systems</a>), that has majority use on smartphones in virtually all countries (all continents have gone Android-majority, including North America<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[274]</a></sup> except for Oceania, because of Australia),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[275]</a></sup> with few exceptions (all of which have <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a>-majority); in the US, Android is close to iOS, having exchanged majority position a few times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[276]</a></sup> Canada and the following are also exceptions: Japan, Philippines, Australia and the only exceptions in Europe are the UK, Switzerland, Belgium and the Nordic countries Denmark, Iceland, Sweden and Norway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[277]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2016, Android was on the majority of smartphones in virtually all countries in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dominating_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-dominating-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[278]</a></sup> excluding United States and Canada (while including North America continent as a whole<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[279]</a></sup>), Australia and Japan. A few countries, such as the UK, lose Android-majority if tablets are included.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adoption_on_tablets">Adoption on tablets</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Front_view_of_Nexus_7_(cropped).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="276" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Front_view_of_Nexus_7_%28cropped%29.png/220px-Front_view_of_Nexus_7_%28cropped%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/83/Front_view_of_Nexus_7_%28cropped%29.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Front_view_of_Nexus_7_(cropped).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nexus_7_(2012_version)" title="Nexus 7 (2012 version)">first-generation Nexus 7</a> tablet, running Android 4.1 Jelly Bean</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite its success on smartphones, initially Android tablet adoption was slow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[280]</a></sup> One of the main causes was the <a href="/wiki/Chicken_or_the_egg" title="Chicken or the egg">chicken or the egg</a> situation where consumers were hesitant to buy an Android tablet due to a lack of high quality tablet applications, but developers were hesitant to spend time and resources developing tablet applications until there was a significant market for them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[281]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tabs_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-tabs-285">[282]</a></sup> The content and app "ecosystem" proved more important than hardware <a href="/wiki/Specification_(technical_standard)" title="Specification (technical standard)">specs</a> as the selling point for tablets. Due to the lack of Android tablet-specific applications in 2011, early Android tablets had to make do with existing smartphone applications that were ill-suited to larger screen sizes, whereas the dominance of Apple's <a href="/wiki/IPad" title="IPad">iPad</a> was reinforced by the large number of tablet-specific <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a> applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tabs_285-1"><a href="#cite_note-tabs-285">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[283]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite app support in its infancy, a considerable number of Android tablets (alongside those using other operating systems, such as the <a href="/wiki/HP_TouchPad" title="HP TouchPad">HP TouchPad</a> and <a href="/wiki/BlackBerry_PlayBook" title="BlackBerry PlayBook">BlackBerry PlayBook</a>) were rushed out to market in an attempt to capitalize on the success of the iPad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tabs_285-2"><a href="#cite_note-tabs-285">[282]</a></sup> <i>InfoWorld</i> has suggested that some Android manufacturers initially treated their first tablets as a "Frankenphone business", a short-term low-investment opportunity by placing a smartphone-optimized Android OS (before Android 3.0 <i>Honeycomb</i> for tablets was available) on a device while neglecting user interface. This approach, such as with the <a href="/wiki/Dell_Streak" title="Dell Streak">Dell Streak</a>, failed to gain market traction with consumers as well as damaging the early reputation of Android tablets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[285]</a></sup> Furthermore, several Android tablets such as the <a href="/wiki/Motorola_Xoom" title="Motorola Xoom">Motorola Xoom</a> were priced the same or higher than the <a href="/wiki/IPad" title="IPad">iPad</a>, which hurt sales. An exception was the <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon</a> <a href="/wiki/Kindle_Fire" title="Kindle Fire">Kindle Fire</a>, which relied upon lower pricing as well as access to Amazon's ecosystem of applications and content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tabs_285-3"><a href="#cite_note-tabs-285">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[286]</a></sup></p>
<p>This began to change in 2012, with the release of the affordable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nexus_7_(2012_version)" title="Nexus 7 (2012 version)">Nexus 7</a> and a push by Google for developers to write better tablet applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[287]</a></sup> According to International Data Corporation, shipments of Android-powered tablets surpassed iPads in Q3 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[288]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of the end of 2013, over 191.6 million Android tablets had sold in three years since 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[290]</a></sup> This made Android tablets the most-sold type of tablet in 2013, surpassing iPads in the second quarter of 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[291]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the StatCounter's June 2015 web use statistics, Android tablets represent the majority of tablet devices used on the <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South American</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[292]</a></sup> (then lost majority) and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> continents (60.23%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[293]</a></sup> while they have equaled with the iPad's market share in major countries on all continents (with the North America as an exception, though in <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a> Android has the majority<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[294]</a></sup>), and getting close to representing the majority on the whole Asian continent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[295]</a></sup> having done so already in India (65.9%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[296]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> (62.22%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[297]</a></sup> and most Middle-Eastern countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[298]</a></sup> In about half of the European countries, Android tablets have a majority market share.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[299]</a></sup> China is an exception for the major developing countries, in which Android <a href="/wiki/Phablet" title="Phablet">phablets</a> (classified as smartphones while similar in size to tablets) are more popular than Android tablets or iPads.</p>
<p>By March 2016, Infoworld stated that Android tablets and smartphones can be a "real part of your business [..] there's no longer a reason to keep Android at arm's length. It can now be as integral to your mobile portfolio as Apple's iOS devices are"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Infoworld2016_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Infoworld2016-21">[19]</a></sup> as they have "business-class apps",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Infoworld2016_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Infoworld2016-21">[19]</a></sup> no longer just "OK for email".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Infoworld2016_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Infoworld2016-21">[19]</a></sup> In 2015, they stated that "Microsoft's Office UI is better on iOS and Android"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Infoworld2015_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Infoworld2015-22">[20]</a></sup> than in Microsoft's own mobile app for Windows.</p>

<p>Charts in this section provide breakdowns of Android versions, based on devices accessing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Play_Store" title="Google Play Store">Google Play Store</a> in a seven-day period ending on January 9, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-android-dev-dashboard_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-android-dev-dashboard-303">[300]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[d]</a></sup> Therefore, these statistics exclude devices running various Android <a href="/wiki/Fork_(software_development)" title="Fork (software development)">forks</a> that do not access the Google Play Store, such as Amazon's <a href="/wiki/Kindle_Fire" title="Kindle Fire">Fire tablets</a>.</p>

<p>Since May 2016, more than half of devices have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OpenGL_ES_3.0" title="OpenGL ES 3.0">OpenGL ES 3.0</a> or higher.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Application_piracy">Application piracy</span></h3>
<p>In general, paid Android applications can easily be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copyright_infringement_of_software" title="Copyright infringement of software">pirated</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piracy_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-piracy-305">[301]</a></sup> In a May 2012 interview with <a href="/wiki/Eurogamer" title="Eurogamer">Eurogamer</a>, the developers of <a href="/wiki/Football_Manager" title="Football Manager">Football Manager</a> stated that the ratio of pirated players vs legitimate players was 9:1 for their game Football Manager Handheld.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[302]</a></sup> However, not every developer agreed that piracy rates were an issue; for example, in July 2012 the developers of the game <a href="/wiki/Wind-up_Knight" title="Wind-up Knight">Wind-up Knight</a> said that piracy levels of their game were only 12%, and most of the piracy came from China, where people cannot purchase apps from Google Play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[303]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, Google released a tool for validating authorized purchases for use within apps, but developers complained that this was insufficient and trivial to <a href="/wiki/Software_cracking" title="Software cracking">crack</a>. Google responded that the tool, especially its initial release, was intended as a sample framework for developers to modify and build upon depending on their needs, not as a finished piracy solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[304]</a></sup> Android "Jelly Bean" introduced the ability for paid applications to be encrypted, so that they may work only on the device for which they were purchased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-appencrypt_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars-appencrypt-309">[305]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[306]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_issues">Legal issues</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oracle_v._Google" title="Oracle v. Google">Oracle v. Google</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smartphone_wars" title="Smartphone wars">Smartphone wars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Patent_troll" title="Patent troll">Patent troll</a></div>
<p>Both Android and Android phone manufacturers have been involved in numerous patent lawsuits. On August 12, 2010, <a href="/wiki/Oracle_Corporation" title="Oracle Corporation">Oracle</a> sued Google over claimed infringement of copyrights and patents related to the <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a> programming language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerworld_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-computerworld-311">[307]</a></sup> Oracle originally sought damages up to $6.1 billion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[308]</a></sup> but this valuation was rejected by a United States federal judge who asked Oracle to revise the estimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[309]</a></sup> In response, Google submitted multiple lines of defense, counterclaiming that Android did not infringe on Oracle's patents or copyright, that Oracle's patents were invalid, and several other defenses. They said that Android's Java runtime environment is based on <a href="/wiki/Apache_Harmony" title="Apache Harmony">Apache Harmony</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Clean_room_design" title="Clean room design">clean room</a> implementation of the Java class libraries, and an independently developed virtual machine called <a href="/wiki/Dalvik_(software)" title="Dalvik (software)">Dalvik</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[310]</a></sup> In May 2012, the jury in this case found that Google did not infringe on Oracle's patents, and the trial judge ruled that the structure of the Java APIs used by Google was not copyrightable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[311]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[312]</a></sup> The parties agreed to zero dollars in <a href="/wiki/Statutory_damages" title="Statutory damages">statutory damages</a> for a small amount of copied code.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-damages_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-damages-317">[313]</a></sup> On May 9, 2014, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Circuit" title="Federal Circuit">Federal Circuit</a> partially reversed the district court ruling, ruling in Oracle's favor on the copyrightability issue, and <a href="/wiki/Remand_(court_procedure)" title="Remand (court procedure)">remanding</a> the issue of <a href="/wiki/Fair_use" title="Fair use">fair use</a> to the district court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnet_appeals_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnet_appeals-318">[314]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cafc_13-1021_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-cafc_13-1021-319">[315]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2015, Google announced that the next major release of Android (<a href="/wiki/Android_Nougat" title="Android Nougat">Android Nougat</a>) would switch to <a href="/wiki/OpenJDK" title="OpenJDK">OpenJDK</a>, which is the official open-source implementation of the Java platform, instead of using the now-discontinued Apache Harmony project as its runtime. Code reflecting this change was also posted to the AOSP source repository.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-njdk_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-ars-njdk-154">[152]</a></sup> In its announcement, Google claimed this was part of an effort to create a "common code base" between Java on Android and other platforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vb-openjdk_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-vb-openjdk-155">[153]</a></sup> Google later admitted in a court filing that this was part of an effort to address the disputes with Oracle, as its use of OpenJDK code is governed under the <a href="/wiki/GNU_General_Public_License" title="GNU General Public License">GNU General Public License</a> (GPL) with a <a href="/wiki/GPL_linking_exception" title="GPL linking exception">linking exception</a>, and that "any damages claim associated with the new versions expressly licensed by Oracle under OpenJDK would require a separate analysis of damages from earlier releases".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-njdk_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-ars-njdk-154">[152]</a></sup> In June 2016, a United States federal court ruled in favor of Google, stating that its use of the APIs was fair use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-googlebeatsoracle_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars-googlebeatsoracle-320">[316]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to lawsuits against Google directly, various <a href="/wiki/Proxy_war" title="Proxy war">proxy wars</a> have been waged against Android indirectly by targeting manufacturers of Android devices, with the effect of discouraging manufacturers from adopting the platform by increasing the costs of bringing an Android device to market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcworld_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-pcworld-321">[317]</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> and Microsoft have sued several manufacturers for patent infringement, with Apple's ongoing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple_Inc._v._Samsung_Electronics_Co.,_Ltd." title="Apple Inc. v. Samsung Electronics Co., Ltd.">legal action against Samsung</a> being a particularly high-profile case. In October 2011, Microsoft said they had signed patent license agreements with ten Android device manufacturers, whose products account for <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.microsoft.com/en-us/news/press/2012/jan12/01-12lgpr.aspx" rel="nofollow">"70% in the U.S."</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAndroid+%28operating+system%29&amp;rft.btitle=70%25+in+the+U.S.&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.microsoft.com%2Fen-us%2Fnews%2Fpress%2F2012%2Fjan12%2F01-12lgpr.aspx&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> and 55% of the worldwide revenue for Android devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10agreements_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-10agreements-322">[318]</a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a> and <a href="/wiki/HTC" title="HTC">HTC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[319]</a></sup> Samsung's patent settlement with Microsoft included an agreement to allocate more resources to developing and marketing phones running Microsoft's Windows Phone operating system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcworld_321-1"><a href="#cite_note-pcworld-321">[317]</a></sup> Microsoft has also <a href="/wiki/Tying_(commerce)" title="Tying (commerce)">tied</a> its own Android software to patent licenses, requiring the bundling of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_Mobile" title="Microsoft Office Mobile">Microsoft Office Mobile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skype" title="Skype">Skype</a> applications on Android devices to subsidize the licensing fees, while at the same time helping to promote its software lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supersite-androidpatents_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-supersite-androidpatents-324">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-xiaomims_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-xiaomims-325">[321]</a></sup></p>
<p>Google has publicly expressed its frustration for the current patent landscape in the United States, accusing Apple, Oracle and Microsoft of trying to take down Android through patent litigation, rather than innovating and competing with better products and services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bully_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-bully-326">[322]</a></sup> In September 2011, Google purchased <a href="/wiki/Motorola_Mobility" title="Motorola Mobility">Motorola Mobility</a> for US$12.5 billion, which was viewed in part as a defensive measure to protect Android, since Motorola Mobility held more than 17,000 patents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moto_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-moto-327">[323]</a></sup> In December 2011, Google bought over a thousand patents from <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arsibm_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-arsibm-328">[324]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, <a href="/wiki/FairSearch" title="FairSearch">FairSearch</a>, a lobbying organization supported by Microsoft, Oracle and others, filed a complaint regarding Android with the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, alleging that its free-of-charge distribution model constituted anti-competitive <a href="/wiki/Predatory_pricing" title="Predatory pricing">predatory pricing</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Free_Software_Foundation_Europe" title="Free Software Foundation Europe">Free Software Foundation Europe</a>, whose donors include Google, disputed the Fairsearch allegations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fsfe_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-fsfe-329">[325]</a></sup> On April 20, 2016, the EU filed a formal antitrust complaint against Google based upon the FairSearch allegations, arguing that its leverage over Android vendors, including the mandatory bundling of the entire suite of proprietary Google software, hindering the ability for competing search providers to be integrated into Android, and barring vendors from producing devices running forks of Android, constituted anti-competitive practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc-euandroidantitrust_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-euandroidantitrust-330">[326]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OUYA-Console-set-h.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3360" data-file-width="5760" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/OUYA-Console-set-h.jpg/220px-OUYA-Console-set-h.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/OUYA-Console-set-h.jpg/330px-OUYA-Console-set-h.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/OUYA-Console-set-h.jpg/440px-OUYA-Console-set-h.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OUYA-Console-set-h.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ouya" title="Ouya">Ouya</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">video game console</a> which runs Android</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The open and customizable nature of Android allows it to be used on other electronics aside from smartphones and tablets, including laptops and <a href="/wiki/Netbook" title="Netbook">netbooks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smartbook" title="Smartbook">smartbooks</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[327]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Smart_TV" title="Smart TV">smart TVs</a> (<a href="/wiki/Android_TV" title="Android TV">Android TV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Google_TV" title="Google TV">Google TV</a>) and cameras (E.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_Camera" title="Galaxy Camera">Galaxy Camera</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[328]</a></sup> In addition, the Android operating system has seen applications on smart glasses (<a href="/wiki/Google_Glass" title="Google Glass">Google Glass</a>), <a href="/wiki/Smartwatch" title="Smartwatch">smartwatches</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[329]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Headphones" title="Headphones">headphones</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[330]</a></sup> car CD and DVD players,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[331]</a></sup> mirrors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[332]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Portable_media_player" title="Portable media player">portable media players</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[333]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Landline" title="Landline">landline</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[334]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/VoIP_phone" title="VoIP phone">Voice over IP phones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[335]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ouya" title="Ouya">Ouya</a>, a video game console running Android, became one of the most successful <a href="/wiki/Kickstarter" title="Kickstarter">Kickstarter</a> campaigns, <a href="/wiki/Crowdfunding" title="Crowdfunding">crowdfunding</a> US$8.5m for its development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ouya_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-ouya-340">[336]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[337]</a></sup> and was later followed by other Android-based consoles, such as <a href="/wiki/Nvidia" title="Nvidia">Nvidia</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Shield_Portable" title="Shield Portable">Shield Portable</a> –  an Android device in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_game_controller" title="Video game controller">video game controller</a> form factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[338]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, Google demonstrated "Android@Home", a home automation technology which uses Android to control a range of household devices including light switches, power sockets and thermostats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[339]</a></sup> Prototype light bulbs were announced that could be controlled from an Android phone or tablet, but Android head Andy Rubin was cautious to note that "turning a lightbulb on and off is nothing new", pointing to numerous failed home automation services. Google, he said, was thinking more ambitiously and the intention was to use their position as a <a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing" title="Cloud computing">cloud</a> services provider to bring Google products into customers' homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[340]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[341]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parrot_(company)" title="Parrot (company)">Parrot</a> unveiled an Android-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Car_stereo" title="Car stereo">car stereo</a> system known as Asteroid in 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-engadget-asteroid_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-engadget-asteroid-346">[342]</a></sup> followed by a successor, the touchscreen-based Asteroid Smart, in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-engadget-asteroidsmart_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-engadget-asteroidsmart-347">[343]</a></sup> In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Clarion_(company)" title="Clarion (company)">Clarion</a> released its own Android-based car stereo, the AX1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[344]</a></sup> In January 2014, at the <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Electronics_Show" title="Consumer Electronics Show">Consumer Electronics Show</a> (CES), Google announced the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Open_Automotive_Alliance" title="Open Automotive Alliance">Open Automotive Alliance</a>, a group including several major automobile makers (<a href="/wiki/Audi" title="Audi">Audi</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyundai" title="Hyundai">Hyundai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Nvidia" title="Nvidia">Nvidia</a>, which aims to produce Android-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In_car_entertainment" title="In car entertainment">in car entertainment</a> systems for automobiles, "[bringing] the best of Android into the automobile in a safe and seamless way."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-autoalliance_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-autoalliance-349">[345]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 18, 2014, Google announced <a href="/wiki/Android_Wear" title="Android Wear">Android Wear</a>, an Android-based platform specifically intended for smartwatches and other wearable devices; only a developer preview was made publicly available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[346]</a></sup> This was followed by the unveiling of two Android-Wear-based devices, the <a href="/wiki/LG_G_Watch" title="LG G Watch">LG G Watch</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moto_360" title="Moto 360">Moto 360</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[347]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 25, 2014, at <a href="/wiki/Google_I/O" title="Google I/O">Google I/O</a>, it was announced that <a href="/wiki/Android_TV" title="Android TV">Android TV</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Smart_TV" title="Smart TV">Smart TV</a> platform, is replacing the previously released <a href="/wiki/Google_TV" title="Google TV">Google TV</a>. Google also announced <a href="/wiki/Android_Auto" title="Android Auto">Android Auto</a> for use in cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[348]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="DESKTOP"></span> Android comes preinstalled on a few laptops (a similar functionality of running Android applications is also available in Google's <a href="/wiki/Chrome_OS" title="Chrome OS">Chrome OS</a>) and can also be installed on <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computers</a> by end users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[349]</a></sup> On those platforms Android provides additional functionality for physical <a href="/wiki/Computer_keyboard" title="Computer keyboard">keyboards</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keyboard_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-keyboard-354">[350]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mouse_(computing)" title="Mouse (computing)">mice</a>, together with the "<a href="/wiki/Alt-Tab" title="Alt-Tab">Alt-Tab</a>" key combination for switching applications quickly with a keyboard. In December 2014, one reviewer commented that Android's notification system is "vastly more complete and robust than in most environments" and that Android is "absolutely usable" as one's primary desktop operating system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[351]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2015, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> reported that Android will serve as Google's future main laptop operating system, with the plan to fold Chrome OS into it by 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chromeconfusion_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-chromeconfusion-356">[352]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-samtran_357-0"><a href="#cite_note-samtran-357">[353]</a></sup> Google's Sundar Pichai, who led the development of Android, explained that "mobile as a computing paradigm is eventually going to blend with what we think of as desktop today."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chromeconfusion_356-1"><a href="#cite_note-chromeconfusion-356">[352]</a></sup> and back in 2009, Google co-founder Sergey Brin himself said that Chrome OS and Android would "likely converge over time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[354]</a></sup> Lockheimer, who replaced Pichai as head of Android and Chrome OS, responded to this claim with an official Google blog post stating that "While we've been working on ways to bring together the best of both operating systems, there's no plan to phase out Chrome OS [which has] guaranteed auto-updates for five years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google:_Chrome_OS_here_to_stay_359-0"><a href="#cite_note-Google:_Chrome_OS_here_to_stay-359">[355]</a></sup> That is unlike Android where support is shorter with "<a href="/wiki/End-of-life_(product)" title="End-of-life (product)">EOL</a> dates [being..] at least 3 years [into the future] for Android tablets for education".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[356]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 40em; -webkit-column-width: 40em; column-width: 40em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Official <a href="/wiki/64-bit_computing" title="64-bit computing">64-bit</a> support for all platforms was introduced in Android 5.0 "Lollipop".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Operating systems for <a href="/wiki/Microcontroller" title="Microcontroller">microcontrollers</a> are a special case, and not considered here, as they are not meant to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_purpose_computer" title="General purpose computer">general purpose computers</a>. They are known to outnumber regular computers, and use specialized real-time operating system (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RTOS" title="RTOS">RTOS</a>), or no operating system at all. All Android devices with network capabilities, also include an RTOS as their second OS.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-266"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-266">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">To put the Statistica's numbers in context: by Strategy Analytics estimates, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Windows" title="Windows">Windows</a> the most popular "desktop" operating system, has an estimated installed base of about 1.3 billion at best;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup> they also estimate the overall tablet installed base to be already of comparable size to the PC market and predict tablets will have surpassed them by 2018.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-304"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-304">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Versions accounting for less than 0.1% are not included.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-Licenses-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Licenses_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://source.android.com/source/licenses.html" rel="nofollow">"Licenses"</a>. <i>Android Open Source Project</i>. Open Handset Alliance<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">September 9,</span> 2012</span>. <q><i>The preferred license for the Android Open Source Project is the Apache Software License, 2.0. ... Why Apache Software License? ... For userspace (that is, non-kernel) software, we do in fact prefer ASL2.0 (and similar licenses like BSD, MIT, etc.) over other licenses such as LGPL. Android is about freedom and choice. The purpose of Android is promote openness in the mobile world, but we don't believe it's possible to predict or dictate all the uses to which people will want to put our software. So, while we encourage everyone to make devices that are open and modifiable, we don't believe it is our place to force them to do so. Using LGPL libraries would often force them to do so.</i></q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAndroid+%28operating+system%29&amp;rft.atitle=Licenses&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fsource.android.com%2Fsource%2Flicenses.html&amp;rft.jtitle=Android+Open+Source+Project.&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 10,</span> 2015</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAndroid+%28operating+system%29&amp;rft.atitle=Sample%3A+hello-jni&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdeveloper.android.com%2Fndk%2Fsamples%2Fsample_hellojni.html&amp;rft.jtitle=developer.android.com&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://godoc.org/golang.org/x/mobile/app" rel="nofollow">"Package app, which lets you write Apps for Android (and eventually, iOS)"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 9,</span> 2015</span>. <q>There are two ways to use Go in an Android App. The first is as a library called from Java, the second is to use a restricted set of features but work entirely in Go.[..] An app can be written entirely in Go. This results in a significantly simpler programming environment (and eventually, portability to iOS), however only a very restricted set of Android APIs are available. The provided interfaces are focused on games. It is expected that the app will draw to the entire screen (via OpenGL, see the go.mobile/gl package), and that none of the platform's screen management infrastructure is exposed. On Android, this means a native app is equivalent to a single Activity (in particular a NativeActivity) and on iOS, a single UIWindow. Touch events will be accessible via this package. When Android support is out of preview, all APIs supported by the Android NDK will be exposed via a Go package.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAndroid+%28operating+system%29&amp;rft.btitle=Package+app%2C+which+lets+you+write+Apps+for+Android+%28and+eventually%2C+iOS%29&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fgodoc.org%2Fgolang.org%2Fx%2Fmobile%2Fapp&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
